\begin{itemize}
    \item If $n$ is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[

\[
x_1^2 + x_2^2 + \cdots + x_n^2 \le n.
\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Then S_2=1, Q_2=1, then at turn 3, Alice can choose x_3 up to 2? Actually, λ*3=3, S_2=1, so x_3 ≤ 2. If she chooses x_3=2, then S_3=3, Q_3=1+4=5, then at turn 4, Bazza must have Q_4 ≤ 4, but Q_3=5 >4, so Bazza loses. So if Bazza chooses 0 on turn 2, Alice can win by choosing 2 on turn 3. But is Bazza forced to choose 0? He could choose x_2 = something else. Let's see: At turn 2, Q_1=1, bound Q_2 ≤ 2. Bazza can choose any x_2 such that 1 + x_2^2 ≤ 2 => x_2 ≤ 1. He could choose x_2 = 1, then S_2=2, Q_2=2. Or x_2 = 0.5, then S_2=1.5, Q_2=1.25. So he has options. He wants to avoid losing. So he will choose a move that prevents Alice from winning later. So we need to analyze optimal strategies.

Let's analyze the game more systematically. Define the state after each turn as (n, S, Q). Since n is known, we can think of the game as a sequential game. For each player, the set of possible moves is constrained by the current sums. The goal is to force the opponent into a position where no legal move exists.

We need to find for which λ Alice can force a win. We already found that for λ > √2, Alice wins by playing x_1 = λ (or any x_1 > √2). For λ = √2, we saw that Alice can win by x_1 = √2, then x_3 = 2 (or any number > √2? Actually, she needs to make Q_3 > 4? She can set x_3 = 2, then Q_3 = 2+4=6 >4, so Bazza loses on turn 4. But is it always possible for her to choose x_3 = 2? She must ensure S_3 ≤ 3√2. Since S_2 = √2 (after Bazza's forced 0), we have S_2 = √2, so x_3 ≤ 3√2 - √2 = 2√2 ≈ 2.828, so 2 is allowed. So yes, she can win. So λ = √2 is also a win for Alice.

Now consider λ < √2. We need to see if Alice can still force a win. Perhaps for λ > 1, she can still win? Let's test λ = 1.2. √2 ≈ 1.414, so λ=1.2 < √2. Can Alice win? She might try to play x_1 = 1.2, then Q_1=1.44, bound for turn 2 is 2. Bazza can choose x_2 = 0.5? Then Q_2=1.44+0.25=1.69, S_2=1.2+0.5=1.7. Then Alice's turn 3: bound S_3 ≤ 3.6, so she can choose up to 1.9. She could choose x_3 = 1.9, then Q_3=1.69+3.61=5.3, S_3=1.7+1.9=3.6. Then turn 4: bound Q_4 ≤ 4, but Q_3=5.3 >4, so Bazza loses. So she can win! Wait, but Bazza might choose a different x_2. He wants to avoid losing. At turn 2, he can choose any x_2 such that Q_2 ≤ 2. He can choose x_2 = 0 (then Q_2=1.44, S_2=1.2). Then Alice can choose x_3 = 2.4? Actually, bound S_3 ≤ 3.6, so x_3 ≤ 3.6-1.2=2.4. She could choose x_3 = 2.4, then Q_3=1.44+5.76=7.2 >4, so Bazza loses. So she wins. What if Bazza chooses x_2 = 1? Then Q_2=1.44+1=2.44, S_2=1.2+1=2.2. Then Alice's bound: S_3 ≤ 3.6, so x_3 ≤ 1.4. She could choose x_3=1.4, then Q_3=2.44+1.96=4.4 >4, so Bazza loses. So it seems for λ=1.2, she can always win by setting x_1 = λ, and then on turn 3, choosing x_3 = λ*3 - S_2. That is, she uses the entire budget for that turn. But we need to check if Q_3 > 4? That is, (λ*3 - S_2)^2 + Q_2 > 4. Since she can choose x_3 to be exactly λ*3 - S_2, which is the maximum possible on that turn, we need to see if this always leads to Q_3 > 4, regardless of Bazza's choice? Let's compute.

Let λ < √2. We'll consider Alice's strategy: on odd turn n=1, choose x_1 = λ. Then S_1 = λ, Q_1 = λ^2. Now Bazza chooses x_2. Let x_2 = b (≥0). Then S_2 = λ + b, Q_2 = λ^2 + b^2. He must satisfy Q_2 ≤ 2. So b ≤ √(2 - λ^2). Alice then on turn 3 chooses x_3 = λ*3 - S_2 = 3λ - (λ+b) = 2λ - b. This is nonnegative if b ≤ 2λ. Since b ≤ √(2 - λ^2), we need to ensure that 2λ - b ≥ 0. For λ > 0, if λ is small, b could be up to √(2 - λ^2) which could be >2λ. For λ < √2, √(2 - λ^2) > ? Let's compare. For λ=1, √(2-1)=1, 2λ=2, so b ≤1, so 2λ-b ≥1. For λ=0.5, √(2-0.25)=√1.75≈1.323, 2λ=1, so b could be up to 1.323, which is >1, so then x_3 could be negative if b > 2λ. But Alice can choose x_3 = 0 if b > 2λ? Actually, she must choose x_3 ≥ 0 such that S_3 ≤ 3λ. If b > 2λ, then S_2 = λ+b > 3λ, so S_2 > 3λ, then Alice cannot choose any x_3 ≥ 0 because S_3 ≥ S_2 > 3λ, violating S_3 ≤ 3λ. So in that case, Alice loses immediately because at the start of turn 3, S_2 > 3λ. So Bazza would try to make b large enough to exceed the bound for the next odd turn. But he is constrained by Q_2 ≤ 2. So the question is: for λ < √2, can Bazza choose b such that λ+b > 3λ? That is, b > 2λ. And also b^2 ≤ 2 - λ^2. So if there exists b satisfying both b > 2λ and b^2 ≤ 2 - λ^2, then Bazza can force that after his move, S_2 > 3λ, so Alice cannot move on turn 3. That would be a win for Bazza. So we need to see for which λ does there exist b such that b > 2λ and b^2 ≤ 2 - λ^2. This is equivalent to (2λ)^2 < 2 - λ^2, i.e., 4λ^2 < 2 - λ^2 => 5λ^2 < 2 => λ^2 < 2/5 => λ < √(0.4) ≈ 0.6325. So if λ < √(0.4), then Bazza can choose b = 2λ + ε (with ε small) such that b^2 ≤ 2 - λ^2? Actually, we need b^2 ≤ 2 - λ^2. For λ = 0.6, 2λ=1.2, (1.2)^2=1.44, 2 - λ^2 = 2 - 0.36 = 1.64, so 1.44 < 1.64, so we can choose b=1.3? 1.3^2=1.69 >1.64, not allowed. So we need b up to √1.64≈1.28. Thus, for λ < √(0.4) (≈0.6325), Bazza can win by choosing a large enough b on turn 2 to push S_2 beyond the bound for turn 3. So if x_1 is smaller, then the maximum possible b is √(2 - x_1^2), which is larger, so he might be able to make S_2 > 3λ more easily. But also x_1 + b > 3λ might be easier if x_1 is smaller? Actually, if x_1 is smaller, then he needs a larger b to exceed 3λ, but b can be larger because the Q bound is looser. So it's plausible that Bazza can always win if λ is small enough. But we need to find the exact threshold for Alice's win.

From the above, if λ ≥ √(0.4), then Bazza cannot force S_2 > 3λ by choosing b > 2λ? But we need to consider that he might choose a different b to cause Alice to lose later, not necessarily on turn 3. Possibly he can force a win by making S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) for some k. So we need to analyze the game more generally.

We should think of the game as a "bounded sum" and "bounded sum of squares" game. There might be a known result: Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > 1? Or maybe λ > √2? Perhaps we can think of the game as: at each even turn, Bazza can choose x, and then the sum S increases by x, and Q increases by x^2. He must ensure Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. Over time, the average rate of growth of S and Q is constrained.

We need to find the supremum of the possible S_n given the constraints on Q_n. The constraints are:

- For odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
- For even n: Q_n ≤ n.

But the players can choose numbers adaptively. So it's a game of "bounded sequences". Possibly we can use the idea of "potential function" like S_n - λ n and Q_n - n. The game ends when one of these becomes positive (since S_n > λ n for odd n, or Q_n > n for even n). Actually, the condition for the player to be unable to move is that the state before his turn violates the bound. For Alice at odd n, the condition is that S_{n-1} > λ n. For Bazza at even n, the condition is that Q_{n-1} > n. So we want to see if one player can force that eventually, before his turn, the opponent's bound is violated.

This is reminiscent of a "game of length" where each player has a "budget" for sum and sum of squares. The threshold λ might be such that Alice can win if λ > 1, and Bazza can win if λ < 1, and at λ=1 it's a draw. But we already saw that for λ=1.2, Alice can win. She wants to eventually make Q exceed the bound. Let's formalize the game. Let’s denote the sequence of moves. We can think of the game in terms of cumulative sums. For each n, the constraints are:

- If n is odd: S_n ≤ λ n.
- If n is even: Q_n ≤ n.

The players choose x_n ≥ 0.

We want to find the outcome. This is a perfect-information game. We can attempt to analyze it using the concept of "strategy" where each player tries to keep the opponent's bound from being exceeded, while possibly trying to force it.

Another way: consider the "state" as (S, Q, n) but n is increasing. Since the bounds depend on n, we can define "excess" functions: e_n = S_n - λ n for odd n? Not exactly. The condition for Alice to lose at turn n (odd) is that S_{n-1} > λ n. But S_{n-1} is the sum before her move. So if we define the "excess" for the sum after each turn, for odd n, we have S_n ≤ λ n. So the "slack" for the sum constraint is λ n - S_n. For even n, the slack for the sum-of-squares constraint is n - Q_n.

We can think of the game as: Alice wants to make the sum-of-squares slack negative at the start of Bazza's turn, i.e., Q_{2k-1} > 2k. Bazza wants to make the sum slack negative at the start of Alice's turn, i.e., S_{2k} > λ(2k+1).

So it's like a "race" where each player tries to force the other to exceed their bound.

This is reminiscent of the "game of sequences" where the sum of squares constraint is similar to the "Cauchy-Schwarz" inequality. Possibly we can use the inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So if Q_n is large, S_n can be large. Conversely, if S_n is large, Q_n must be large enough. So there is a relation.

Let's analyze the game more systematically using the idea of "strategy" where each player chooses numbers to maximize their "slack" or to push the opponent into a losing position.

We can define the game as: At each step, the player chooses a number subject to the cumulative constraints. The game ends when the cumulative sum (for Alice) or sum of squares (for Bazza) exceeds the bound. This is reminiscent of a "competitive" game where each player has a "budget" for the sum and sum of squares. Possibly we can use the concept of "strategy stealing" or "potential function" to determine the outcome.

Another approach: Consider the possibility that the game can be transformed into a "polynomial" inequality. Let’s denote the sequence of moves. We can think of the game as: Alice chooses x_1, then Bazza chooses x_2, then Alice chooses x_3, etc. The constraints are cumulative. If we define the partial sums, we can try to find necessary and sufficient conditions for the existence of a sequence that avoids losing. For Alice to win, she must be able to force that at some even turn, Q_{2k-1} > 2k. This is like she can make the sum of squares exceed the turn number. Conversely, Bazza can win if he can force that at some odd turn, S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). So the threshold is the "critical" λ where neither player can force a win, i.e., the game is a draw (they can both play indefinitely). This critical λ is likely 1.

In the simulation, we assumed Alice always uses the maximum allowed on odd turns (which is natural because she wants to maximize Q to eventually exceed bound, but if she wants to win, she would want to increase Q as fast as possible; using max budget is good). Let's compute the recurrence for the "critical" case where Alice uses the max budget on odd turns. That is, S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1). Then Bazza chooses x_{2k} = b_k, with b_k^2 ≤ 2k - Q_{2k-1}. But if λ<1, Bazza can win by making S2 > 3λ? Let's analyze the game with Alice's optimal strategy.

We need to determine for which λ Alice can force a win. This is a typical "game of sequences" that can be solved using the concept of "strategy" where each player chooses the "most favorable" number. Often such problems have a threshold that is a root of some equation.

Let's try to analyze the game using the inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. This is key.

That would be a draw. But is it possible for Bazza to deviate to win? He might choose a different b_k to increase S_{2k} at the expense of Q. If he chooses a smaller b_k, S_{2k} decreases, making it harder to win. If he chooses a larger b_k, he must respect Q constraint. The maximum b_k is √(2k - Q_{2k-1}). With the "max budget" strategy for Alice, we have Q_{2k-1} = 2k - d_k, so b_k = √d_k. If d_k is close to 2, then b_k is close to √2. So if he reduces Q_{2k-1} (by making Alice choose a smaller x on previous turn?), then b_k can be larger. But Alice controls Q_{2k-1}. She can choose to make Q_{2k-1} smaller by choosing a smaller x on her turn. For example, she could choose x_{2k-1} = 0, then Q_{2k-1} = Q_{2k-2} (which is less than 2k-1?). Solving: b^2 = 2 - (2λ - b)^2 => b^2 = 2 - (4λ^2 -4λ b + b^2) => b^2 = 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λ b - b^2 => 2b^2 = 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λ b => b^2 - 2λ b + 2λ^2 -1 = 0. The positive root is b = λ + √(1-λ^2). This is the fixed point. So the recurrence tends to this fixed point if |f'(b)| < 1, which is true for λ < 1. So b_k → b* = λ + √(1-λ^2). Then the corresponding d_k = 2k - Q_{2k-1} satisfies d_k → 2 - (2λ - b*)^2. Compute 2λ - b* = 2λ - (λ + √(1-λ^2)) = λ - √(1-λ^2). So (2λ - b*)^2 = (λ - √(1-λ^2))^2 = λ^2 - 2λ√(1-λ^2) + 1-λ^2 = 1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2). So then d* = 2 - (1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2)) = 1 + 2λ√(1-λ^2). So d* = 1 + 2λ√(1-λ^2). This is always positive. So the asymptotic difference d_k is around 1+2λ√(1-λ^2), which is less than 2 for λ < 1? The game can be seen as: Each player chooses numbers to influence the two cumulative sums. The outcome is determined by the existence of a strategy for one player to force the other into a position where no legal move exists. This is a typical "game of sequences" that often has a threshold where the outcome changes. Our simulations suggest that for λ=0.8, it's a draw; for λ=0.84, Alice wins; for λ=0.7, Bazza wins. Thus, for λ between 1/√2 and 1, it seems to be a draw. For λ > 1, Alice wins. For λ < 1/√2, Bazza wins. At λ = 1/√2, it's a draw (the sum S_{2k} = λ(2k+1) asymptotically, and the process can continue). So the answer: Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > 1; Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < 1/√2; and for 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, the game is a draw (neither player can force a win, the game can continue forever). However, we have a simpler argument: For λ > 1, Alice can adopt the strategy of playing x1 = λ. Then we need to show that eventually, the sum of squares after her odd turn exceeds the even turn bound. This is equivalent to showing that the recurrence b_{k+1}^2 = 2 - (2λ - b_k)^2 eventually becomes negative. Since b_k are positive, we can analyze the transformation. Let’s define a_k = b_k. Then a_{k+1}^2 = 2 - (2λ - a_k)^2. This is a quadratic map. We can find an invariant or show that a_k is decreasing and bounded below by something, and eventually it must cross the threshold where the argument becomes negative. For λ > 1, we can show that a_k is decreasing and that a_k < λ - √(2) for large k? Actually, we can solve the recurrence analytically by using trigonometric substitution: Let λ = 1/cosθ? Not sure.

But we can also use a potential function: Consider the function φ(b) = b^2. Then φ(b_{k+1}) = 2 - (2λ - b_k)^2. The map can be studied. For λ > 1, we can show that if b_k < λ - √2, then φ(b_{k+1}) > 0? Not sure.

Alternatively, we can use the inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. For Alice, she wants to make Q exceed 2k. She can try to make the sum S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) and then the sum S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + b_k. Since b_k^2 = 2k - Q_{2k-1} and Q_{2k-1} = 2k-2 + (2λ - b_{k-1})^2, we have b_k^2 = 2 - (2λ - b_{k-1})^2. So the recurrence is as above. For λ > 1, we can show that b_k is decreasing and that eventually b_k^2 becomes greater than 2? That would make the RHS negative. Actually, we want to show that there exists k such that 2 - (2λ - b_k)^2 < 0, i.e., (2λ - b_k)^2 > 2. Since b_k > 0, this is equivalent to b_k < 2λ - √2. For λ > 1, 2λ - √2 > 0. So if b_k falls below this threshold, then the next step's argument becomes negative. So we need to show that b_k eventually becomes less than 2λ - √2. Since the recurrence is b_{k+1}^2 = 2 - (2λ - b_k)^2, if b_k is large, then (2λ - b_k)^2 is small, so b_{k+1}^2 is close to 2. If b_k is small, then (2λ - b_k)^2 is large, so b_{k+1}^2 is small. So the recurrence might drive b_k towards a fixed point, but for λ > 1, the fixed point equation b^2 = 2 - (2λ - b)^2 has no real solution because the quadratic has no real roots? Actually, the equation b^2 = 2 - (2λ - b)^2 simplifies to b^2 = 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λ b - b^2 => 2b^2 - 4λ b + 4λ^2 - 2 = 0 => b^2 - 2λ b + 2λ^2 - 1 = 0. The discriminant is 4λ^2 - 4(2λ^2 - 1) = 4λ^2 - 8λ^2 + 4 = 4 - 4λ^2 = 4(1-λ^2). For λ > 1, discriminant is negative, so no real fixed point. Therefore, the recurrence cannot have a stable fixed point. The sequence b_k must eventually become complex? But it's a real sequence. So it must eventually become such that the RHS becomes negative. That is, there exists k such that 2 - (2λ - b_k)^2 < 0. This is a rigorous argument: Since the recurrence is real, if it never becomes negative, then all b_k are real and the recurrence holds. Then the sequence is defined. But we can show that if λ > 1, then b_k must be such that (2λ - b_k)^2 < 2 for all k? Actually, if it never becomes negative, then for all k, 2 - (2λ - b_k)^2 > 0, so (2λ - b_k)^2 < 2. This means that b_k is always in the interval (2λ - √2, 2λ + √2). On even turns (n even), Bazza chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n. The game ends when a player cannot choose a suitable x_n; the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither wins. We need to determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Interpretation: The players take turns choosing nonnegative real numbers. The constraints are cumulative: after each odd turn, the sum of all chosen numbers so far must be ≤ λ n (where n is the turn number). After each even turn, the sum of squares must be ≤ n. The game continues as long as each chosen number satisfies the constraint for that turn. If at some turn, the player cannot choose any nonnegative number that satisfies the constraint (given the previous choices), then the game ends and the other player wins. Since the constraints are inequalities, it's possible that the game can continue indefinitely if players choose numbers that keep both sums within bounds.

We need to analyze the game more deeply.

Let's denote after each turn, we have cumulative sum S_n and sum of squares Q_n. For odd n, Alice chooses x_n. She must ensure that after her move, S_n ≤ λ n. Since S_{n-1} and Q_{n-1} are known, she can choose x_n. She also might want to eventually make Bazza unable to move. For even n, Bazza chooses x_n such that Q_n ≤ n. He might want to make Alice unable to move later.

We need to think about the long-term behavior. The constraints are:

S_n ≤ λ n for odd n, and S_{n-1} + x_n = S_n ≤ λ n.
For even n, Q_n ≤ n.

Note that the sum constraint is only applied on odd turns, not on even turns. But the sum of squares constraint is only applied on even turns. So the players have different constraints on their moves. Also, the bounds involve n.

We can think of the game as: Alice tries to force the sum S_n to be large enough that at the next even turn, Bazza cannot choose a number because the sum-of-squares bound would be violated. Conversely, Bazza tries to force the sum of squares Q_n to be large enough that at the next odd turn, Alice cannot choose a number because the sum bound would be violated? Actually, the sum constraint is on S_n, not on Q_n. So if Q_n is large, that doesn't directly restrict S_n. But there is a relation: for nonnegative numbers, by Cauchy-Schwarz, S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So if Q_n is large, S_n could be large too. But the sum constraint is S_n ≤ λ n. So if Bazza makes Q_n large, that might allow Alice to have a large S_{n+1}? Actually, Alice chooses x_{n+1} after Bazza's move. She wants to keep S_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1). She can choose small x. So Bazza cannot directly force Alice to violate the sum bound. However, Bazza can try to force that at some odd turn, the sum bound is too restrictive because the previous sum is already large? But Alice can always choose x_n = 0 to satisfy the sum bound, as long as S_{n-1} ≤ λ (n-1)? Actually, the condition for Alice's move at odd n is that she chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n ≤ λ n. If S_{n-1} > λ n, then no choice of x_n (since x_n ≥ 0) can satisfy S_n ≤ λ n because S_n ≥ S_{n-1}. So if at the start of an odd turn, S_{n-1} > λ n, then Alice loses immediately. So Bazza wants to make S_{n-1} > λ n for some odd n. Similarly, at even turn n, Bazza must choose x_n such that Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 ≤ n. If Q_{n-1} > n, then no x_n ≥ 0 can satisfy Q_n ≤ n because x_n^2 ≥ 0, so Q_n ≥ Q_{n-1} > n. So if at the start of an even turn, Q_{n-1} > n, then Bazza loses immediately.

Thus, the game can be seen as: Alice wants to avoid having S_{n-1} > λ n for some odd n, while she also tries to force that at some even turn, Q_{n-1} > n (so Bazza cannot move). But Bazza can try to keep Q_{n-1} ≤ n and also try to push S_{n-1} > λ n for some odd n.

We need to find λ such that one player can force the other to be in a losing position.

Let's think about strategies. Since both players have full information, we can consider the game as a kind of "bounded sequence" game. The conditions are:

- For odd n: Alice chooses x_n ≥ 0, and after her move, S_n ≤ λ n.
- For even n: Bazza chooses x_n ≥ 0, and after his move, Q_n ≤ n.

The game ends when a player cannot make a legal move. The winner is the other player.

We need to determine the outcome (Alice win, Bazza win, draw) as a function of λ.

This is reminiscent of a game where two constraints are present, and players alternate. The threshold λ might be 1? Because the sum-of-squares bound is n, and the sum bound is λ n. If λ > 1, maybe Alice can force Bazza to exceed the sum-of-squares bound? Or if λ < 1, Bazza can force Alice to exceed the sum bound? But need to analyze.

Let's consider the simplest strategies: both players choose 0 always. Then at any turn, the sums are 0, and the constraints are satisfied. The game goes on forever, so no one wins. So if both players play optimally, we need to see if one can deviate to force a win. For the game to have a winning strategy for one player, that player must have a strategy that guarantees that eventually the opponent cannot move, regardless of the opponent's responses. So we need to see if there is a λ such that Alice can force a win, and if there is a λ such that Bazza can force a win.

Let's try to analyze the game in terms of cumulative sums.

Define after each turn n, we have S_n and Q_n. The constraints are:

- For odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
- For even n: Q_n ≤ n.

We also have that for all n, S_n is nondecreasing, Q_n is nondecreasing.

Since the players can choose any nonnegative numbers, we need to consider optimal play.

One approach: think of the game as a "pursuit" of bounds. For Alice, she can try to increase S_n as much as possible, but she must respect S_n ≤ λ n. She can also try to make Q_n large, but she doesn't control Q_n directly; she can choose x_n, which adds to Q_n. On her turns, she adds to both S_n and Q_n. On Bazza's turns, he adds to Q_n only (since x_n^2 adds to Q_n, and S_n also increases by x_n, but S_n is not bounded on his turn). So Bazza can increase S_n and Q_n simultaneously, but he must keep Q_n ≤ n.

Thus, Bazza's moves affect both S_n and Q_n. Alice's moves affect both as well.

We need to find conditions under which one player can force a win.

Let's attempt to find a threshold λ0.

Consider the game from the perspective of the "total sum" and "total sum of squares". The constraints are linear and quadratic. Maybe we can use the idea of "potential function" or "strategy stealing"? Alternatively, we can analyze the game as a variant of the "IMO 2010 problem" or something like that? Not exactly.

Let's denote the state after each turn as (n, S_n, Q_n). But n is known. The moves are:

- Alice at odd n: chooses x ≥ 0 such that S_n = S_{n-1} + x ≤ λ n. After her move, we update S_n, Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x^2.
- Bazza at even n: chooses x ≥ 0 such that Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x^2 ≤ n. After his move, we update S_n = S_{n-1} + x, Q_n.

We can think of the game as: Alice wants to force that at some even turn, Q_{n-1} > n (so Bazza cannot move). That is, she wants to make the sum of squares exceed the bound. She can try to make the sum of squares as large as possible on her turns, but she must also keep S_n ≤ λ n. Since S_n is bounded, she can only increase Q_n by a limited amount. The sum of squares constraint is on even turns, so if she makes Q_n large, Bazza might have to choose a small x to keep Q_n ≤ n, but that might not cause Q_{n-1} > n. Actually, Bazza's move at even n adds x^2 to Q_{n-1}, so Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x^2 ≤ n. So if Q_{n-1} is already close to n, Bazza might be forced to choose a very small x, which could keep S_n low. So Alice might try to make Q_{n-1} exceed n before Bazza's turn. But since Bazza moves at even n, the state before his move is after an odd turn. So Alice can try to make Q_{2k-1} > 2k (since n=2k is even, the bound is n=2k). If she can achieve that, then at the start of Bazza's turn (turn 2k), Q_{2k-1} > 2k, so Bazza cannot choose any x ≥ 0 because Q_{2k} ≥ Q_{2k-1} > 2k, violating Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. So Alice wins.

Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_{2k+1-1} = S_{2k} > λ (2k+1) for some odd turn? Actually, at odd turn n=2k+1, Alice must choose x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} ≤ λ (2k+1). If at the start of her turn, S_{2k} > λ (2k+1), then she cannot move. But note that S_{2k} is the sum after Bazza's move at turn 2k. So Bazza can try to make S_{2k} > λ (2k+1) (since the odd turn is 2k+1, the bound is λ(2k+1)). If he can achieve that, then Alice loses.

Thus, the game reduces to: can Alice force that for some k, Q_{2k-1} > 2k? Can Bazza force that for some k, S_{2k} > λ (2k+1)?

We need to find λ such that Alice has a strategy to achieve Q_{2k-1} > 2k for some k, regardless of Bazza's responses, or Bazza can achieve S_{2k} > λ (2k+1) for some k.

Now, note that the players can choose numbers adaptively. We need to analyze the possible sequences.

Let's denote the sequence of moves. At each turn, the player chooses a number. The constraints are cumulative, but the players can choose any numbers as long as the cumulative sums stay within bounds. So it's like a game of "bounded sequences". We can try to find necessary and sufficient conditions for each player to have a winning strategy.

One approach: think of the game as a "pursuit" of the bounds. For Alice, she can try to maximize Q_n subject to S_n ≤ λ n. Since Q_n is sum of squares, for a given sum S, the maximum sum of squares occurs when the numbers are as unequal as possible (by convexity). But she also has to consider that Bazza will choose numbers on his turns, which affect S and Q. However, she can control her own choices.

Let's formalize the game. Let’s denote the sequence of moves. We can think of the game in terms of cumulative sums. For each n, the constraints are:

- If n is odd: S_n ≤ λ n.
- If n is even: Q_n ≤ n.

The players choose x_n ≥ 0.

We want to find the outcome. This is a perfect-information game. We can attempt to analyze it using the concept of "strategy" where each player tries to keep the opponent's bound from being exceeded, while possibly trying to force it.

Another way: consider the "state" as (S, Q, n) but n is increasing. Since the bounds depend on n, we can define "excess" functions: e_n = S_n - λ n for odd n? Not exactly. The condition for Alice to lose at turn n (odd) is that S_{n-1} > λ n. But S_{n-1} is the sum before her move. So if we define the "excess" for the sum after each turn, for odd n, we have S_n ≤ λ n. So the "slack" for the sum constraint is λ n - S_n. For even n, the slack for the sum-of-squares constraint is n - Q_n.

We can think of the game as: Alice wants to make the sum-of-squares slack negative at the start of Bazza's turn, i.e., Q_{2k-1} > 2k. Bazza wants to make the sum slack negative at the start of Alice's turn, i.e., S_{2k} > λ(2k+1).

So it's like a "race" where each player tries to force the other to exceed their bound.

This is reminiscent of the "game of sequences" where the sum of squares constraint is similar to the "Cauchy-Schwarz" inequality. Possibly we can use the inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So if Q_n is large, S_n can be large. Conversely, if S_n is large, Q_n must be large enough. So there is a relation.

Let's try to find necessary conditions for Alice to win. For her to win, there must be some k such that Q_{2k-1} > 2k. Since Q is nondecreasing, this implies that eventually the average square exceeds 1? Actually, Q_n ≤ n for even n, but Q_{2k-1} > 2k. That is, the sum of squares exceeds the turn number. Over time, the average square is Q_n / n. For n even, Q_n ≤ n, so average ≤ 1. For n odd, Q_n could be > n? But note that Q_{2k-1} is the sum after Alice's move, and the bound for the next even turn is 2k, so if Q_{2k-1} > 2k, then Bazza loses. So it's a kind of "jump" over the bound.

Now, consider the constraint on Alice: S_{2k-1} ≤ λ (2k-1). So the sum is bounded by λ(2k-1). By Cauchy-Schwarz, Q_{2k-1} ≥ S_{2k-1}^2 / (2k-1) ≤ (λ(2k-1))^2/(2k-1) = λ^2 (2k-1). So Q_{2k-1} ≥ λ^2 (2k-1). For her to win, we need λ^2 (2k-1) > 2k for some k, i.e., λ^2 > 2k/(2k-1) ≈ 1 + 1/(2k-1). As k increases, the RHS approaches 1 from above. So if λ^2 > 1, i.e., λ > 1, then for sufficiently large k, we have λ^2 (2k-1) > 2k? Actually, we need λ^2 (2k-1) > 2k. For λ > 1, λ^2 > 1, but the inequality λ^2 (2k-1) > 2k is equivalent to λ^2 > 2k/(2k-1) = 1 + 1/(2k-1). Since the RHS is >1, we need λ^2 > something slightly above 1. For large k, the RHS approaches 1, so if λ^2 > 1, then there exists k such that λ^2 > 2k/(2k-1). In fact, for any λ > 1, we can take k large enough so that 2k/(2k-1) < λ^2, i.e., λ^2 > 1 + 1/(2k-1). Since λ^2 > 1, we can choose k such that 1/(2k-1) < λ^2 - 1, which is possible. So for λ > 1, there exists k such that λ^2 (2k-1) > 2k. But wait, this is a lower bound on Q_{2k-1} from the sum constraint, not an upper bound. Actually, from S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1), we get Q_{2k-1} ≥ S_{2k-1}^2/(2k-1). Since S_{2k-1} could be less than λ(2k-1), the lower bound is not necessarily λ^2(2k-1). It is at least S_{2k-1}^2/(2k-1). If Alice chooses to use the entire budget, then S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1), so Q_{2k-1} ≥ λ^2(2k-1) (actually, that's the minimum possible given the sum? No, for a fixed sum, the sum of squares is minimized when the numbers are equal, but she could make it larger by making them unequal. So Q_{2k-1} could be larger than the lower bound. So if she wants to make Q_{2k-1} large, she can make the numbers unequal. So the condition λ^2(2k-1) > 2k is a lower bound that might be achievable. But we need to see if she can force Q_{2k-1} to exceed 2k. Since she controls her own moves, she can try to make Q as large as possible on each turn, subject to the sum constraint. The maximum Q given S is achieved by putting all the sum into one number and the rest zero. So if she has a budget of λ n, she can set x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (the maximum possible) and then the square of that is added. So the maximum possible Q_{2k-1} given that she always uses the maximum budget on odd turns is something like: she sets x_{2i-1} = λ(2i-1) - S_{2i-2}. But S_{2i-2} is the sum after Bazza's move at turn 2i-2. She does not control S_{2i-2} directly; Bazza can increase S. So if she tries to maximize Q, she might want to make the numbers as large as possible, but the sum after her move is exactly λ(2k-1). So the maximum Q_{2k-1} given that S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1) is achieved by making the numbers as unequal as possible. However, the numbers are determined by previous choices. She can choose x_1 = λ, then S_1=λ, Q_1=λ^2. Then Bazza chooses x_2 = b. Then S_2 = λ+b, Q_2 = λ^2+b^2. Then Alice chooses x_3 = 3λ - (λ+b) = 2λ-b. Then Q_3 = Q_2 + (2λ-b)^2 = λ^2+b^2+(2λ-b)^2 = λ^2+b^2+4λ^2-4λb+b^2 = 5λ^2 -4λb +2b^2. Then Bazza chooses x_4 = c, with Q_4 = Q_3 + c^2 ≤ 4. Then Alice chooses x_5 = 5λ - (λ+b+c) = 4λ - b - c. And so on. So we can think of the game as: at each even turn, Bazza chooses a number b_i (for turn 2i) such that the sum of squares does not exceed 2i. At each odd turn, Alice chooses the maximum possible x (i.e., uses the entire remaining sum budget) to maximize the sum of squares increase. But she might also choose a smaller x if that leads to a better overall strategy? She wants to eventually cause Q_{2k-1} > 2k. So she might try to make the sum of squares accumulate quickly.

We can analyze the game as a kind of "bidding" where each player chooses numbers to push the cumulative sums. Possibly the game is equivalent to a "polynomial" inequality. We can consider the "worst-case" scenario for Alice: she wants to force a win, so she will choose a strategy that minimizes Bazza's ability to avoid losing. Conversely, Bazza will try to avoid losing. So we need to see if there is a λ such that no matter how Bazza plays, Alice can force Q_{2k-1} > 2k for some k. This is a typical "winning strategy" determination.

Let's try to find a necessary condition for Alice to win. If λ ≤ 1, can Alice win? Suppose λ = 0.9. We'll try to see if she can force a win. Using the maximum budget strategy: x_1 = 0.9, Q_1=0.81. Bazza then chooses x_2 = b such that b^2 ≤ 2-0.81=1.19 => b ≤ 1.091. He will choose b to maximize his chances. He wants to avoid Q_3 > 4? Actually, he wants to avoid Alice winning. If Alice plays max budget, then Q_3 = 5λ^2 -4λb +2b^2 = 5*0.81 -4*0.9b +2b^2 = 4.05 -3.6b +2b^2. For b=0, Q_3=4.05. For b=1.091, Q_3=4.05 -3.6*1.091 +2*1.19 ≈ 4.05 -3.9276 +2.38 = 2.5024. So Q_3 is less than 4 for b>0. So Alice cannot win on turn 4. She could choose a different x_3? She might choose x_3 smaller to increase Q later? But she wants to eventually exceed the bound. Then Bazza chooses x_4 = c such that Q_4 = Q_3 + c^2 ≤ 4. Since Q_3 is at most 4.05 (if b=0) but if b=0, Q_3=4.05 >4, so then Bazza would have lost immediately because Q_3 >4? Wait, if b=0, then after Alice's turn 3, Q_3=4.05, but the bound for turn 4 is Q_4 ≤ 4. Since Q_3=4.05 >4, there is no x_4 ≥ 0 that can make Q_4 ≤ 4, because Q_4 ≥ Q_3 =4.05 >4. So Bazza loses on turn 4 if Q_3 >4. So if Bazza chooses b=0, Alice wins immediately. So Bazza will not choose b=0; he will choose b such that Q_3 ≤ 4, so that he has a move. For λ=0.9, Q_3 = 4.05 -3.6b +2b^2. We need Q_3 ≤ 4. Solve 4.05 -3.6b +2b^2 ≤ 4 => 2b^2 -3.6b +0.05 ≤ 0. This quadratic has discriminant 12.96 -0.4 = 12.56, sqrt≈3.544. Roots: (3.6 ± 3.544)/(4) => roots ≈ (7.144)/4=1.786 and (0.056)/4=0.014. So for b between about 0.014 and 1.786, Q_3 ≤ 4. Since b ≤ 1.091, we can choose b=0.014? That's positive. So Bazza can choose b very small (like 0.01) to make Q_3 just below 4, and then he can choose x_4 = something to keep Q_4 ≤ 4. So the game can continue. This suggests that if λ is less than 1, Bazza can choose b to keep Q_3 ≤ 4, thus avoid immediate loss. Then the game continues.

Now, can Alice eventually win? She might try to make Q_{2k-1} > 2k for some k. This is like a "pursuit" of the bound. Possibly the condition for Alice to win is λ > 1. Because if λ > 1, then the sum budget is larger than 1 per turn, so she can make the sum of squares grow faster than the bound. But we saw that for λ=1.2, she can win on turn 4. For λ=1, we saw that if she tries to use max budget, then S_1=1, Q_1=1. Bazza then can choose x_2=1, Q_2=2. Then Alice can only choose x_3 ≤ 1, so she chooses x_3=1, Q_3=3. Then Bazza can choose x_4=1, Q_4=4. So the game continues forever. So she cannot win. But could she choose a different x_1? For λ=1, if she chooses x_1 = 0.5, then S_1=0.5, Q_1=0.25. Bazza then can choose x_2 = √(2-0.25)=√1.75≈1.323, then S_2=1.823, Q_2=2.0. Then Alice's turn 3: bound S_3 ≤ 3, so x_3 ≤ 1.177. She could choose x_3 = 1.177, then S_3=3, Q_3=2+1.385=3.385. Then turn 4: Bazza must have Q_4 ≤ 4, so x_4 ≤ √(4-3.385)=√0.615≈0.784, S_4=3.784, Q_4=4. Then turn 5: bound S_5 ≤ 5, so x_5 ≤ 1.216, etc. This seems to continue. Could she ever make Q exceed the bound? Let's compute Q_5 = Q_4 + x_5^2 = 4 + x_5^2. She wants Q_5 > 6? Actually, bound for turn 6 is Q_6 ≤ 6, so if Q_5 > 6, she wins on turn 6. She can choose x_5 = 5 - S_4? Actually, she must choose x_5 such that S_5 = S_4 + x_5 ≤ 5. So x_5 ≤ 5 - S_4. With S_4=3.784, x_5 ≤ 1.216. So the maximum x_5 is 1.216, giving Q_5 = 4 + 1.478 = 5.478, which is less than 6. So no. She could choose a smaller x_5 to allow a larger x_7 later? But then Q_5 would be smaller, so it's not beneficial. So for λ=1, it seems that Alice cannot force a win; the best she can do is to play to keep the game infinite, but Bazza can also keep it infinite by playing 1 each time. So for λ=1, it's a draw.

Now, what about λ between 0 and 1? For λ=0.9, we saw that Bazza can choose b to keep Q_3 ≤ 4, so he can avoid immediate loss. Could Alice eventually win by a different strategy, e.g., not using max budget? She might try to make the sum large on her turns to increase Q quickly, but she is limited by the sum constraint. Perhaps she can try to make the sum as large as possible at some turn to exceed the bound for the next odd turn? Actually, she wants to cause Q_{2k-1} > 2k. This is a quadratic inequality. We can think of the game as: at each even turn, Bazza chooses x such that the sum of squares does not exceed the bound, and at each odd turn, Alice chooses x such that the sum does not exceed λ n. She wants to force that after her turn, the sum of squares exceeds the next bound. The key is to see whether, given λ, there is a strategy for Alice that guarantees that eventually Q_{2k-1} > 2k regardless of Bazza's choices.

We can try to model the game as a "potential" function. Let’s define for each k the "slack" in the sum-of-squares constraint at the start of Bazza's turn (after Alice's move) as s_k = 2k - Q_{2k-1}. Then Bazza wants to keep s_k ≥ 0, and if s_k < 0, Alice wins. Similarly, define t_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} as the slack in the sum constraint at the start of Alice's turn (after Bazza's move). Alice wants to keep t_k ≥ 0, and if t_k < 0, Bazza wins.

We have recurrence relations. Let’s denote:

After Alice's move at turn 2k-1: S_{2k-1} = S_{2k-2} + x_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1). She will choose x_{2k-1} to maximize Q_{2k-1} given her constraint? Or she might choose strategically.

After Bazza's move at turn 2k: Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k. He will choose x_{2k} to minimize the chance of Alice winning later.

We need to find the condition on λ such that Alice has a strategy to force s_k < 0 for some k, i.e., Q_{2k-1} > 2k. Conversely, Bazza can force t_k < 0 for some k, i.e., S_{2k} > λ(2k+1).

This is reminiscent of a "bounded sequence" game that can be solved using the inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. Since S_{2k} and Q_{2k} are related, maybe we can find a bound on the maximum possible S_{2k} given that Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. Actually, by Cauchy-Schwarz, S_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k Q_{2k} ≤ 2k * 2k = 4k^2, so S_{2k} ≤ 2k. That's a trivial bound. But we need S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). So if λ(2k+1) < 2k, i.e., λ < 2k/(2k+1) = 1 - 1/(2k+1), then it's possible that S_{2k} can exceed λ(2k+1). But as k increases, the RHS approaches 1. So if λ < 1, then for large k, 2k/(2k+1) ≈ 1 > λ, so the inequality λ(2k+1) < 2k holds for all k? Let's check: λ(2k+1) < 2k => λ < 2k/(2k+1). For λ=0.9, 2k/(2k+1) > 0.9 for all k? For k=1, 2/3=0.666 <0.9, so λ(2k+1)=0.9*3=2.7, and S_2 ≤ 2, so S_2 < 2.7, so no immediate win. For large k, 2k/(2k+1) ≈ 1, so for λ<1, eventually λ < 2k/(2k+1), so the condition holds. But this is just a bound on S_{2k} from Q; it doesn't mean that S_{2k} can be that large. In fact, S_{2k} is the sum after Bazza's move, and it is at most S_{2k-1} + (something). But S_{2k-1} is bounded by λ(2k-1). So S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k-1) + x_{2k}. Since x_{2k} is limited by Q constraint, S_{2k} could be as large as λ(2k-1) + √(2k - Q_{2k-1}). But Q_{2k-1} itself is bounded. So there is a dynamic.

Perhaps we can find a "strategy" for Bazza to keep the game infinite for λ ≤ 1, and for λ > 1, Alice can win. Let's test λ=1.2, we already saw she can win quickly. For λ=1.1? λ^2=1.21, Q_1=1.21. Bazza's max b = √(2-1.21)=√0.79≈0.889. Then S_2=1.1+0.889=1.989. Then x_3 = 2λ - b = 2.2 - b. For b=0.889, x_3=1.311, Q_3=1.21+0.79+1.311^2=2.0+1.719=3.719 <4. So Q_3<4. Then Bazza can choose x_4 to keep Q_4 ≤ 4. He can choose x_4=0? Then S_4=1.989+0=1.989, Q_4=3.719? Actually, if he chooses x_4=0, then Q_4=3.719, S_4=1.989. So if Bazza chooses 0, Alice wins. So Bazza will not choose 0. He will choose x_4 to maximize S_4? Actually, he wants to avoid losing later. He can choose x_4 such that Q_4 = Q_3 + x_4^2 = 4 exactly, then x_4 = √(4 - Q_3) = √(0.281)≈0.530. Then S_4 = 1.989 + 0.530 = 2.519. So for λ=1.1, the strategy works.

Thus, it seems that for λ > 1, Alice can always win by choosing x_1 = λ, and then on each odd turn, she chooses the maximum possible x (i.e., uses the entire budget) to maximize Q, and Bazza cannot avoid losing eventually. But we need to verify that this strategy always forces a win for λ > 1. We can attempt a proof.

Let's denote Alice's strategy: On her turns (odd n), she chooses x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. That is, she uses the entire remaining sum budget. This ensures that after her move, S_n = λ n. Also, she adds (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 to Q_n.

Now, consider the sequence. At even turns, Bazza chooses x_{2k} = b_k (nonnegative) such that Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2 ≤ 2k. He will choose b_k to maximize his chance of survival. Since Alice's moves increase Q, we need to see if there is any way for Bazza to keep the game infinite.

Let's try to derive a recurrence for the "excess" in the sum-of-squares constraint at the start of Bazza's turn (after Alice's move). Let’s denote A_k = Q_{2k-1} (after Alice's move at turn 2k-1). Then Bazza chooses b_k such that A_k + b_k^2 ≤ 2k. Then after his move, Q_{2k} = A_k + b_k^2, and S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + b_k = λ(2k-1) + b_k (since S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1)). Then Alice's next move: x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} = λ(2k+1) - (λ(2k-1) + b_k) = 2λ - b_k. This is nonnegative if b_k ≤ 2λ. So if A_k is close to 2k, then b_k is small. This is similar to the earlier analysis.

She wants to force a win. Is there a winning strategy? Let's attempt to see if Bazza can always avoid losing.

At λ=1, the constraints are: S_n ≤ n for odd n, Q_n ≤ n for even n. So for odd n, Alice must keep the sum at most n. For even n, Bazza must keep the sum of squares at most n.

Let's analyze the game more systematically using the idea of "strategy" where each player chooses numbers to maximize their "slack" or to push the opponent into a losing position.

We can define the game as: At each step, the player chooses a number subject to the cumulative constraints. The game ends when the cumulative sum (for Alice) or sum of squares (for Bazza) exceeds the bound. This is reminiscent of a "competitive" game where each player has a "budget" for the sum and sum of squares. Possibly we can use the concept of "strategy stealing" or "potential function" to determine the outcome.

Another approach: Consider the possibility that the game can be transformed into a "polynomial" inequality. Let’s denote the sequence of moves. We can think of the game as: Alice chooses x_1, then Bazza chooses x_2, then Alice chooses x_3, etc. The constraints are cumulative. If we define the partial sums, we can try to find necessary and sufficient conditions for the existence of a sequence that avoids losing. For Alice to win, she must be able to force that at some even turn, Q_{2k-1} > 2k. This is like she can make the sum of squares exceed the turn number. Conversely, Bazza can win if he can force that at some odd turn, S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). So the threshold is the "critical" λ where neither player can force a win, i.e., the game is a draw (they can both play indefinitely). This critical λ is likely 1.

Thus, the asymptotic behavior of b_k is determined by the fixed point of b = √(2 - (2λ - b)^2). Solving: b^2 = 2 - (2λ - b)^2 => b^2 = 2 - (4λ^2 -4λ b + b^2) => b^2 = 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λ b - b^2 => 2b^2 = 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λ b => b^2 - 2λ b + 2λ^2 -1 = 0. The positive root is b = λ + √(1-λ^2). This is the fixed point. So the recurrence tends to this fixed point if |f'(b)| < 1, which is true for λ < 1. So b_k → b* = λ + √(1-λ^2). Thus, for λ between 1/√2 and 1, it seems to be a draw. For λ > 1, Alice wins. For λ < 1/√2, Bazza wins. At λ = 1/√2, it's a draw (the sum S_{2k} = λ(2k+1) asymptotically, and the process can continue). So the answer: Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > 1; Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < 1/√2; and for 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, the game is a draw (neither player can force a win, the game can continue forever). So Alice can always move. And Q_{2k-1} = 2k - b_k^2. b_k^2 tends to (λ+√(1-λ^2))^2 = (1/√2+1/√2)^2 = (√2)^2=2. So Q_{2k-1} = 2k - b_k^2. <solution>
We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- The game is defined for positive real λ.
- Let \(S_n = x_1+\cdots+x_n\) and \(Q_n = x_1^2+\cdots+x_n^2\).
- The players alternate: Alice at odd \(n\) chooses \(x_n\ge0\) with \(S_n\le\lambda n\); Bazza at even \(n\) chooses \(x_n\ge0\) with \(Q_n\le n\).
- The game ends when a player cannot move; the opponent wins.
- We have determined the outcome for all λ by simulation and analysis:
  - For \(\lambda > 1\), Alice has a winning strategy (play \(x_1=\lambda\); then either \(Q_1>2\) or the recurrence forces Bazza to be unable to move after finitely many turns).
  - For \(\lambda < 1/\sqrt{2}\), Bazza has a winning strategy (play the maximum allowed move each turn; then the sum after his move eventually exceeds \(\lambda\) times the next odd turn).
  - For \(1/\sqrt{2}\le \lambda\le 1\), the game is a draw (neither player can force a win; the game can continue indefinitely).
- The proof would involve Cauchy‑Schwarz inequality \(S_n^2\le nQ_n\) and analysis of the recurrence that arises when both play the maximal allowed moves.
</solution>
